        googletag.enableServices();
    });

            apstag.fetchBids({timeout: 2000}, function (bids) {
                apstag.setDisplayBids();
                googletag.pubads().refresh();
            });
        });

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d84480db414a4b7e8edf409a55b22a38">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f7b76472-c81e-4d75-b42b-234db4e78f28'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      </div>
   </div>
   <div class="epub-sections"><div class="epub-section"><span class="epub-state">First published: </span><span class="epub-date">24 April 2017</span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d144533134" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Layered metal oxide</b> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (M=Co, Mn, Fe) microspheres have been synthesized via a facile self-templated method as advanced cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. As an example, the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit greatly enhanced electrochemical sodium storage properties with high reversible capacity (125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability and long-term cycle life (86 % capacity retention over 300 cycles).
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/71dfa3d0-1167-4a91-8a43-2d30c522031b/anie201702024-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/71dfa3d0-1167-4a91-8a43-2d30c522031b/anie201702024-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/475380c6-76d6-4c90-96c9-3875cac2d497/anie201702024-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/71dfa3d0-1167-4a91-8a43-2d30c522031b/anie201702024-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d144533197" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered metal oxides have attracted increasing attention as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the application of such cathode materials is still hindered by their poor rate capability and cycling stability. Here, a facile self-templated strategy is developed to synthesize uniform P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres. Due to the unique microsphere structure, the contact area of the active material with electrolyte is minimized. As expected, the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit enhanced electrochemical performance for sodium storage in terms of high reversible capacity (125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability and long cycle life (86 % capacity retention over 300 cycles). Importantly, the synthesis method can be easily extended to synthesize other layered metal oxide (P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub>) microspheres.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201702024-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Rapid growth of renewable electric energy from solar, wind and other sources in the global energy markets has spurred the development of low-cost and effective energy storage systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0001_R_d144533013e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0004_R_d144533013e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> As lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) expand their territory from portable electronics to electric vehicles, a great concern arises about the widespread availability and rising price of lithium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0005_R_d144533013e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0006_R_d144533013e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Therefore, exploration of alternatives to LIBs has been highlighted in recent years and a number of battery systems such as lithium-sulfur,<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0007_R_d144533013e363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0009_R_d144533013e363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span> lithium-air<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0010_R_d144533013e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0013_R_d144533013e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Chem.">13</a></sup></span> and rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs)<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0014_R_d144533013e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0017_R_d144533013e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span> have been revisited as possible candidates of post Li-ion technologies. Among them, SIBs have been considered as an ideal choice due to their similar chemical/electrochemical reaction mechanisms to LIBs, low cost and abundant sodium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0016_R_d144533013e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">16</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0018_R_d144533013e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0019_R_d144533013e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>SIBs have attracted increasing attention in the past decade and many materials have been explored as electrodes for SIBs. However, due to the larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> ion compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ion, it is a more arduous challenge to explore suitable electrode materials with robust structure for stable reversible Na<sup>+</sup> ion extraction and insertion. As for the development of next-generation LIBs, the development of practical SIBs is also limited by the lack of suitable cathode materials. Among the potential cathode materials, phosphates,<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0020_R_d144533013e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0022_R_d144533013e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a></sup></span> ferrocyanides<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0023_R_d144533013e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">23</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0025_R_d144533013e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></sup></span> and layered metal oxides<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0026_R_d144533013e385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0029_R_d144533013e385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a></sup></span> have been intensively investigated, but their overall performance is still generally unsatisfactory for practical use of SIBs. Layered metal oxides have been considered as a class of promising cathode materials for SIBs, due to the rich family and various electrochemical active elements.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0014_R_d144533013e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a></sup></span> The layered metal oxides can be catalogued into two main groups: O3 and P2 type structures, where Na<sup>+</sup> ions tend to locate at octahedral and prismatic sites, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0027_R_d144533013e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci.">27</a></sup></span> O3 type oxides possess a stable structure with reversible Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion/deinsertion, but the reversible capacity can hardly exceed 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Although P2 type oxides can deliver higher capacity, the cycling stability is usually less satisfactory due to the multi-phases transition during electrochemical reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0030_R_d144533013e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Mater.">30</a></sup></span> Up to now, the investigation of layered metal oxides mainly focuses on the adjustment of the active metal center and ratio, determination of reaction mechanisms and structural analysis. However, very few attention has been paid to the morphological effect on electrochemical performance for cathode materials of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0015_R_d144533013e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Rev.">15</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0027_R_d144533013e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci.">27</a></sup></span> It is known that cathode materials with regular morphology and microsize could endow the electrodes with high capacity, long-term cyclability and high energy density for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0031_R_d144533013e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0034_R_d144533013e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span> For example, Hwang et al. have reported an O3 type layered compound with a radially aligned hierarchical columnar structure in spherical particles showing high specific capacity, good capacity retention and rate capability for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0032_R_d144533013e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Commun.">32</a></sup></span> Zhang et al. have synthesized graphene decorated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> microspheres, which enable high rate capability with long cycle life for sodium storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0031_R_d144533013e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span> These results indicate that cathode materials with microsphere structure indeed lead to enhanced sodium storage performance. On the other hand, microspheres can also improve the volumetric energy density of the electrodes and more importantly can be easily processed to obtain uniform electrode films, which are very favorable for practical batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Herein we present a facile strategy to synthesize uniform P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres through a two-step self-templating method, as schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0001">1</a>. First, CoCO<sub>3</sub> microspheres synthesized via a modified solvothermal method are converted to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres by calcination in air. Then, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres react with an appropriate amount of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> under elevated temperature to obtain P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres. Although high calcination temperature is used during the synthesis process, the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> particles can still maintain the spherical morphology. Impressively, the resultant P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit excellent sodium storage properties with high reversible capacity, superior rate capability and cycling stability. The synthesis method has been also successfully extended to synthesize P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> microspheres.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201702024-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/69181e70-865d-4901-b5fc-761b9ab68cff/anie201702024-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/69181e70-865d-4901-b5fc-761b9ab68cff/anie201702024-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a47fdb08-5641-4921-aa8f-fdf9cc258e67/anie201702024-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/69181e70-865d-4901-b5fc-761b9ab68cff/anie201702024-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201702024-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201702024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration of the two-step synthesis of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The panoramic field-emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) image (Figure S1a, Supporting Information (SI)) shows that the as-synthesized CoCO<sub>3</sub> precursor consists of microspheres with an average diameter of about 5 μm. The magnified FESEM image (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> a) reveals the rough surface of the CoCO<sub>3</sub> microspheres. After being annealed in air, the CoCO<sub>3</sub> precursor microspheres are transformed into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres, as confirmed by FESEM (Figure S1 b, SI) and powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements (Figure S1 c, SI). Compared with the CoCO<sub>3</sub> precursor, no obvious changes in structure and size can be observed for the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> b). After the second step calcination with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> at elevated temperature, phase-pure Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> with good crystallinity is obtained (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> c). All the diffraction peaks of the XRD pattern can be indexed to a hexagonal P2-structure Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> with <i>P</i>63/<i>mmc</i> space group (JCPDS card No. 87-0274).<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0035_R_d144533013e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a></sup></span> As demonstrated in the diagrammatic presentation (inset of Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> c), the P2 type layered structure exhibits an AB BA oxygen packing with Co ions in the octahedral sites and Na ions in the prismatic sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0036_R_d144533013e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a></sup></span> The large-scale FESEM image of the product shows that the particles exhibit a morphology of well-maintained microspheres (Figure S2, SI). This sample of as-obtained Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres is denoted as <i>s</i>-NCO. Clearly, the surface structure of <i>s</i>-NCO (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> d,e) is different from that of the CoCO<sub>3</sub> and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres, which should originate from the recrystallization and crystal growth during the high temperature calcination. The high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) image (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0002">2</a> f) shows a clear lattice with a spacing of 0.541 nm, which matches well with the (002) plane of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. The TEM image (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0003">3</a> a) demonstrates that the surface of the microsphere is rather rough. The corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) analysis (inset in Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0003">3</a> a) shows that the microsphere is composed of Na, Co and O elements with a Na/Co atomic ratio of ca. 0.71, further confirming the chemical composition of Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. Elemental mapping of a part of the microsphere (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0003">3</a> b–d) reveals that Na, Co and O elements are uniformly distributed in the particle. In addition, to understand the effect of morphology on the electrochemical performance of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> material, P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microparticles with irregular morphology (donated as <i>i</i>-NCO) are also synthesized and evaluated as cathode materials for SIBs (Figure S3, SI).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201702024-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/91792d81-4461-45db-a44e-81bcefe89e5f/anie201702024-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/91792d81-4461-45db-a44e-81bcefe89e5f/anie201702024-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/30793b18-767a-4cb0-807a-54087e06f83a/anie201702024-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/91792d81-4461-45db-a44e-81bcefe89e5f/anie201702024-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201702024-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201702024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>FESEM images of a) CoCO<sub>3</sub> and b) Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> microspheres. c) XRD pattern of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres; inset is the corresponding crystal structure of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. d,e) FESEM images and f) HRTEM image of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201702024-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/80294b43-193f-4765-8f7c-abab4b4289d4/anie201702024-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/80294b43-193f-4765-8f7c-abab4b4289d4/anie201702024-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85ecec72-25ba-416d-8a81-ec059c3ca26e/anie201702024-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/80294b43-193f-4765-8f7c-abab4b4289d4/anie201702024-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201702024-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201702024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Typical TEM image of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres and corresponding elemental mapping of b) sodium, c) cobalt, and d) oxygen elements. The inset in (a) is the EDX spectrum of P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The sodium storage properties of <i>s</i>-NCO are first evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV). Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> a shows a typical CV curve of <i>s</i>-NCO at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.0–3.8 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Similar to most P2 type oxides, the <i>s</i>-NCO cathode exhibits multiple pairs of redox peaks, indicating a series of complex phase transition reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0027_R_d144533013e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci.">27</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0036_R_d144533013e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0037_R_d144533013e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span> The complicated phase transition comes from the changeable states of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and MO<sub>2</sub> layer gliding during Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/deinsertion.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0036_R_d144533013e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0038_R_d144533013e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochim. Acta">38</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> b shows the representative charge/discharge voltage profiles of <i>s</i>-NCO between 2.0 and 3.8 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a current rate of 0.04 C (5 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 1 C=125 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The discharge profiles exhibit multiple potential drops separated by either potential plateaus or sloping curves in good agreement with the CV result and reported literatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0036_R_d144533013e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0038_R_d144533013e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochim. Acta">38</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0040_R_d144533013e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">40</a></sup></span> The voltage plateaus indicate the existence of biphasic domains which are the characteristic of first-order transitions during Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/deinsertion. The sloping curves are related to the solid-solution behavior and potential drops are the signature of single-phase in a narrow sodium content range.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0036_R_d144533013e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a></sup></span> Importantly, the <i>s</i>-NCO cathode can deliver a reversible discharge capacity of 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of most of the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> electrodes reported previously.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0035_R_d144533013e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0040_R_d144533013e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">40</a>-<a href="#anie201702024-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0042_R_d144533013e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Ceram. Int.">42</a></sup></span> The improved specific capacity may result from the high crystallinity and phase purity of the Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0043_R_d144533013e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0044_R_d144533013e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Ionics">44</a></sup></span> Moreover, the <i>s</i>-NCO cathode offers an average discharge potential of 2.9 V, possessing a high energy density of 363 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201702024-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87fe80b2-c435-4569-9b95-9b32293e5287/anie201702024-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/87fe80b2-c435-4569-9b95-9b32293e5287/anie201702024-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f77c9ff0-af54-4bf0-b710-9982c71cec24/anie201702024-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87fe80b2-c435-4569-9b95-9b32293e5287/anie201702024-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201702024-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201702024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) A typical cyclic voltammogram of the <i>s</i>-NCO electrode at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Typical charge/discharge voltage profiles of the <i>s</i>-NCO electrode at a current rate of 0.04 C. c) Rate performance of <i>s</i>-NCO and <i>i</i>-NCO, and d) the corresponding charge/discharge voltage profiles of the <i>s</i>-NCO electrode at various current rates. e) Cycling performance of <i>s</i>-NCO and <i>i</i>-NCO at a current rate of 0.4 C and the corresponding Coulombic efficiency of <i>s</i>-NCO. (Note: 1 C=125 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.)</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The rate performance of the <i>s</i>-NCO cathode is investigated by charging and discharging at various current rates ranging from 0.04 to 16 C (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> c). As can be seen, the electrode can deliver an average reversible capacity of 125, 121, 119, 114, 112, 109, 106, 98 and 84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.04, 0.08, 0.16, 0.4, 0.8, 1.6, 2.4, 4 and 8 C, respectively. Even at a high rate of 16 C (charge/discharge within about 2 min), a remarkable reversible and stable capacity of 64 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be preserved. When the current density is reduced back to 0.16 C, a reversible capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be recovered, revealing the excellent cycling reversibility of <i>s</i>-NCO. Also shown in Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> c, the discharge capacity of the <i>i</i>-NCO electrode fades rapidly as the current rate rises and the capacity drops to almost zero at the current rate of 4 C. The excellent rate capability of the <i>s</i>-NCO is also verified by the charge/discharge voltage profiles at various current rates (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> d).</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the <i>s</i>-NCO electrode also exhibits excellent cycling stability. Specifically, a capacity retention of 86 % over 300 cycles at a current rate of 0.4 C is observed (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> e), corresponding to an average capacity loss of 0.046 % per cycle. The Coulombic efficiency of the electrode remains around 100 % during cycling. For comparison, the capacity of the <i>i</i>-NCO cathode drops rapidly with cycling and a capacity of only 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is maintained after 200 cycles, corresponding to a low capacity retention of 46 % (Figure <a href="#anie201702024-fig-0004">4</a> e). Impressively, the <i>s</i>-NCO cathode also exhibits excellent cycling performance at other current rates (Figure S4, SI), retaining more than 88 % of the initial capacity after 200 cycles (Table S1, SI). To the best of our knowledge, such superior rate capability and long cycling stability have not been reported before for P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathodes. The superior sodium storage properties of <i>s</i>-NCO may be attributed to its high crystallinity and uniform microsphere structure. The microsphere structure can effectively buffer the mechanical stress caused by Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/deinsertion. It can also minimize the contact area of the electroactive materials with electrolyte, thus reducing unwanted side reactions and active materials dissolution and leading to high Coulombic efficiency.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0032_R_d144533013e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Commun.">32</a></sup></span> It is also worth mentioning that the measured tap density of <i>s</i>-NCO (1.8 g cm<sup>−3</sup>) is higher than that of <i>i</i>-NCO (1.45 g cm<sup>−3</sup>), which is expected to improve the volumetric energy density of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Importantly, this self-templated synthesis method is quite general and can be also applied to prepare other layered metal oxide microspheres, such as P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> microspheres. All the Mn/Fe carbonates, Mn/Fe oxides, and as-derived Mn/Fe layered oxides show a similar microsphere structure (Figure S5, SI). The corresponding XRD patterns (Figure S6 a,b, SI) confirm the successful synthesis of the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> phases from corresponding metal carbonate precursors. EDX analysis (Figure S6 c,d, SI) confirms the elemental compositions of the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> microspheres. The as-obtained P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> microspheres also exhibit enhanced reversible capacity (Figure S7, SI) compared to the materials in previous studies.<span><sup><a href="#anie201702024-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0045_R_d144533013e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eur. J.">45</a>, <a href="#anie201702024-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201702024-bib-0046_R_d144533013e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Mater.">46</a></sup></span> In addition, it can be seen that the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> electrodes exhibit different charge/discharge profiles with the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> electrode. This can be attributed to the different electron configurations of the active elements (Co, Mn, Fe), leading to different stable intermediates with complex changeable states of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering, charge ordering and MO<sub>2</sub> layer gliding during Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/deinsertion.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully developed a facile and self-templated method to synthesize Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (M=Co, Mn, Fe) layered metal oxide microspheres. This method first involves the conversion of metal carbonates to metal oxide microspheres. Then the metal oxides are converted to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> microspheres after reaction with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> at elevated temperature. As an example, the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres are thoroughly evaluated as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Benefiting from their structural advantages, the P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit high specific capacity (125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent rate capability and long-term cycling stability (86 % retention in capacity over 300 cycles). This work might offer some inspiration for development of advanced cathode materials for practical deployment of SIBs.</p>
